Learning to type like a grown up

by Andy Appleton
Monday, 03 February 2014

Over the last year I=E2=80=99ve slowly developed hand and wr= ist pain from long stints at the keyboard. I recently decided that enough i= s enough and I need to take it seriously if I want to be able to do the job= I love for the rest of my career. My first naive attempt at a fix was to p= urchase an ergonomic split keyboard. I went for the Microsoft Sculpt er= gonomic after reading some good reviews, particularly Marco Arment=E2= =80=99s.

This actually made things worse and it didn=E2=80=99t take me long to fi= gure out that the problem was my typing technique. Like many people of my g= eneration I was not taught touch typing and have never felt the motivation = to learn independently. I was using two fingers per hand and making some we= ird shapes as I reached for different key combos. The rotated hand position= on the split keyboard accentuated these movements which led to more pain. = On top of that I realised that I needed to look at the keyboard out of the = bottom of my eyes as I typed =E2=80=93 the split layout hid the keys from v= iew which led to a lot of missed keys.

Doing it properly

It was Christmas and I had a few days off work so I decided to stop maki= ng excuses and learn to touch type. I went cold turkey on my old technique = and started following the course on = typingclub.com. It was hugely frustrating for about two days until I ha= d worked through the lessons for all of the main characters but I was able = to become reasonably productive within half a week, albeit at a glacial 20 = words per minute.

I=E2=80=99m now a month in and am able to type fluently without looking = the keyboard and often without consciously thinking about the location of i= ndividual keys. My speed is up in the 35-40 WPM range and I=E2=80=99m more = accurate too. I=E2=80=99ve pretty much completed the typing club course but= I keep revisiting the speed test lessons to try and edge up my speed and a= ccuracy. I now absolutely love my ergonomic keyboard =E2=80=93 it keeps my = wrists at a neutral angle and is really comfortable. If you secretly know t= hat your typing technique sucks you owe it to yourself to learn touch typin= g. You will be frustrated for a few days at worst and will be faster and mo= re comfortable within a month.

What about the wrist pain?

Touch typing is just one helpful factor in trying to eliminate the pain I=E2=80=99ve been experiencing this last month I=E2=80= =99ve also done a lot of reading and have visited a doctor. A big change is= that I=E2=80=99ve started using Break= Time app to force me to take a 2 minute break every 20 minutes [1]. This = lets me stretch out my hands regularly to make sure they=E2=80=99re not cra= mped in the same position for long periods. I=E2=80=99ve also made a consci= ous effort to not use a keyboard unless it=E2=80=99s really necessary, I=E2= =80=99m trying to keep away from the computer at the weekend and in the eve= ning to give my hands time to rest and recover.

The doctor tells me that I can expect it to take 6-8 months to fully rec= over but I=E2=80=99m starting to feel a bit better already. If you=E2=80=99= re dealing with hand or wrist pain I would strongly recommend doing some re= ading (this page fro= m Clay Scott is very thorough) and making some changes to your work set= up. Mine is not too bad and hopefully I=E2=80=99ve made good changes early = enough that it won=E2=80=99t become a longterm problem, I strongly encourag= e you to do the same.

  1. I was expecting this to kill productivity but as a programmer I actuall= y find it a good opportunity to think about whether what I'm doing is reall= y correct. It's easy to blindly dive down the rabbit hole of a suboptimal s= olution and the breaks are a great way to counter that. =E2=86=A9

Taking Ember for a spin

by Andy Appleton
Friday, 25 October 2013

After Ember.js hit 1.0 r= ecently I decided to give it a fair shot and try it out for a week in the c= ontext of an app I=E2=80=99m working on. The app actually uses Angular, which I=E2=80=99m very comfortable with = but it=E2=80=99s always good to evaluate alternatives and be able to pick a= tool based on experience.

I=E2=80=99m building a little recipe/shopping list app. The idea is that= you can generate a shopping list by selecting recipes you=E2=80=99d like t= o cook and how many people it should serve. The ingredients are summed up t= o create a list which you can take to the shops.

This list page has a few interesting features:

  • Tapping/clicking a single item will strike a line through it. The sta= te of checked and unchecked items is persisted in localStorage= only.
  • There is a toggle to show and hide already checked items.
  • When all items are checked and the toggle is set to hide checked item= s, a complete screen shows.

I already had this page implemented in Angular so I knew how it should w= ork and I figured it would be a good comparison. I created a new git branch= , deleted all of the Angular code and jumped into reimplementing it with Em= ber. Here=E2=80=99s a loose collection of my conclusions.

ember-data

I found a lot to like about Ember. In Ember data it has a very solid mod= el and persistence abstraction which I really appreciated coming from Angul= ar which has next to nothing.

It provides a store object in the browser which you make queries against= . Either it already contains the data you requested or it makes a round tri= p to the server to retrieve it, caching for next time. Either way it return= s a promise so the interface is consistent. Over the life of a long running= single page app I can see this saving a lot of needless requests.

Models declare associations to other models and attribute types are decl= ared upfront. I much prefer this to the Angular way of just parsing server = JSON into a plain JavaScript object regardless of content. In the past I=E2= =80=99ve encountered bugs where server JSON changed subtly and broke some f= ront end functionality in a way I didn=E2=80=99t spot until it was too late= . Ember expects attributes to be present and of the correct type and throws= helpful errors when they=E2=80=99re not.

Ember.Component

Ember=E2=80=99s answer to Angular directives and an attempt to forward p= atch web component s= upport. Ember.Component does a lot less than Angular=E2=80=99s directive, b= ut I think it is a great example of providing 80% of the power with much le= ss learning overhead.

All Ember components get a new controller and there is only one way of s= coping the controller. Contrast with Angular where you must learn about iso= late and inherit scopes, transclusion and linking/compile functions before = you can be productive =E2=80=94 directives are seen as an advanced topic in= Angular, components are not in Ember and I think that is to its immense cr= edit.

I implemented each list item as component and handled the localSto= rage persistence inside the individual controllers =E2=80=94 it was = easy to get started with, especially having some prior knowledge of web com= ponents and Angular directives.

Naming Conventions

Ember relies heavily on naming conventions. Your controllers and templat= es must be named just so to be constructed and rendered correctly and all o= bjects must be placed into a single app namespace object.

I prefer the flexibility of a module system for declaring dependencies a= nd would prefer not to rely on naming conventions, which can be easily typo= ed. The flip side is that module systems (particularly Angular=E2=80=99s) a= dd a lot of boilerplate code to a file which Ember just doesn=E2=80=99t nee= d.

Testing

The Ember docs are excellent in almost all ways, but there is no informa= tion about testing which I found quite disappointing. The information I was= able to find seemed more focused on integration level tests so I=E2=80=99d= love to see a post or some docs on unit test best practices. This is one a= rea where Angular really shines and it gives a lot of confidence putting an= app into production knowing that it it well tested.

Summary

Overall I=E2=80=99ve really enjoyed working with Ember. I=E2=80=99m far = more productive with Angular, but then I=E2=80=99m also far more experience= d. I=E2=80=99m going to finish building my shopping list app with Angular b= ut for my next project I=E2=80=99ll seriously consider Ember as an alternat= ive. I think Ember will really shine with a more data-rich application and = I=E2=80=99m looking forward to what they can do with server side pre-render= ing.

If you=E2=80=99re looking to get started with Ember, here=E2=80=99s some= resources which helped me out:

  • Ember introduction screencast=
  • Ember guides
  • Ember API reference
  • Discourse (a l= arge open source Ember app) source code

Diving into AngularJS

by Andy Appleton
Sunday, 21 April 2013

This weekend I=E2=80=99ve had some spare time and I=E2=80=99= ve used it to take a proper look at Angul= arJS. I=E2=80=99ve used Backbone= a lot and find myself defaulting to it as soon as a JavaScript application= gets complicated. I realised that I was ignoring everything else because I= already know Backbone and that=E2=80=99s not a good place to be. Time to j= ump into something new.

When all you have is a hammer=E2= =80=A6

Initially I downloaded the Angular source and launched into trying to bu= ild something simple with it but I found this a really frustrating experien= ce. I consider myself a pretty competent JS developer but nothing really wo= rked and the concepts didn=E2=80=99t click. Making me feel stupid is a quic= k way to turn me off of something.

I took a step back and realised that I was trying so hard to shoehorn wh= at I knew about MVC from Rails and Backbone into the app that I wasn=E2=80= =99t letting myself see the bigger picture. I spent an hour watching and co= ding along with a tutorial = video and by the end felt I really understood the basics. Just by inves= ting a small amount of time into learning the fundamentals I=E2=80=99m now = really productive with Angular. Making me feel smart is a great wa= y to keep me interested in something!

In less than a day I=E2=80=99ve come to love the declarative nature of t= he code I=E2=80=99m writing. Everything is so terse and I=E2=80=99m spendin= g zero time writing what I would now consider boilerplate DOM manipulation = or event binding code. This is really exciting coming from a world of manua= l template rendering and zombie events and I love not having to think about= that stuff.

Conventions

I listened to an inte= rview with Chad Fowler this week. He spoke about how Rails is not neces= sarily the best web framework but it offers such a strong set of convention= s and has such a strong community that it really allows you to forget about= hooking an app together and just focus on the product you=E2=80=99re tryin= g to build.

I feel like Angular offers the same level of convention and I=E2=80=99m = really looking forward to getting stuck into a bigger project with it. I do= n=E2=80=99t want to spend my time writing glue code, clearing up old views,= manually re-rendering templates on data change or writing form input handl= ers. I know there are libraries to handle these things in Backbone, but I a= lso don=E2=80=99t want to be depending on 20 plugins just to get a function= ing app.

Step back

These are just my initial thoughts after playing with the framework for = a day and maybe I=E2=80=99ll change my mind after I=E2=80=99ve used it more= extensively but right now I=E2=80=99m loving Angular and can=E2=80=99t see= myself choosing Backbone the next time I start a big front end JS project.=

If you=E2=80=99re looking to get started with Angular I=E2=80=99d recomm= end you check out the following resources:

  • Dan Whalin=E2=80=99s = video tutorial really helped me to get the fundamentals of Angular.
  • egghead.io is a great collecti= on of screencasts covering some more specific cases.
  • The AngularJS project homepage = (although the docs are not that beginner friendly).

Responsive Web Design is not a war

by Andy Appleton
Thursday, 07 March 2013

Recently there=E2=80=99s been a bit of a furore around a post written = after we at GoCardless launched our red= esigned homepages. I wanted to make a few points =E2=80=93 to be clear = these are my thoughts, and they=E2=80=99re not necessarily shared by my col= leagues.

Responsive Web D= esign is not always appropriate

Elliot Jay Stocks says =E2=80=9Cthe war has not = yet been won=E2=80=9D. I think that=E2=80=99s a rather black and wh= ite way to look at a much more nuanced issue and that painting it as a war = is not especially helpful. As with everything on the web there=E2=80=99s de= grees of appropriateness.

In a lot of cases, a well thought out responsive site is awesome. A grea= t example would be a blog (go on, resize the window on this page). In other= cases though it=E2=80=99s totally ill-suited.

Take the example of the GoCardless h= omepage. There=E2=80=99s a fair amount of information here and not ever= yone wants to see all of it. By setting a small screen=E2=80=99s zoom level= to 100% and inlining all of the content we=E2=80=99re forcing the user to = scroll (and scroll and scroll) through potentially useless information. In = my opinion it=E2=80=99s much better to provide a high level overview and le= t the user zoom into the interesting sections.

I appreciate that Elliot is arguing that it=E2=80=99s not all about mobi= le but honestly which device are you using that has a very narrow screen bu= t can=E2=80=99t zoom and pan?

Let=E2=80=99s look at GoCardless on an iPhone as an example:

Which is a better experience? Which gives the user a quicker understandi= ng of what=E2=80=99s happening?

I think online maps are a good analogy. You wouldn=E2=80=99t open Google= Maps, zoom in as far as possible and then start panning around to find the= area you want to look at. Instead you look at the overview, pan around unt= il you=E2=80=99re in broadly the right place and then start to focus in.

In many cases, especially marketing and homepages, this is how I would l= ike to use websites on a mobile device and I bet I=E2=80=99m not alone.

Obviously it=E2=80=99s not all bad

That said, there=E2=80=99s clearly a place for responsive design. I= =E2=80=99m not saying that it=E2=80=99s a fad and should b= e ignored. Blogs look great as a phone-width column of text, I wouldn= =E2=80=99t want to be zooming and panning around GMail on my phone and that= =E2=80=99s just two examples off the top of my head.

All I=E2=80=99m saying is that as with everything there=E2=80=99s a time= and a place and that I often find myself wishing I could see the full site= rather than the scaled down responsive version I=E2=80=99m presented with.= Let=E2=80=99s not go off the deep end with talk of wars and treat this as = the nuanced issue that it is.

Prefilling Backbone model data

by Andy Appleton
Monday, 31 December 2012

The Backbon= e docs suggest that you should bootstrap data into your page on load. T= his allows the application to be responsive and ready to use as soon as the= JavaScript is loaded. This is not always possible though =E2=80=93 for exa= mple if the app does not have any kind of server side templating or if it i= nteracts with an external API. (DailyJS has a good example of this kind of application).

In this case the application can seem slow to start as it first loads th= e JavaScript and then makes one or many API calls to populate the interface= with data. Caching can help to a degree but this does not allow for new da= ta to be fetched.

Cache Prefilling

I have a plugin called Backbone fetch cache which provides an in-memory and localStorage cache for Backbone=E2=80=99s fetch method. I=E2=80=99= ve recently added a prefill option which will return cached da= ta immediately (where available) but still make the background API request.=

This is really nice because it allows us to present the user with a work= ing interface immediately and then update the data in place as the AJAX req= uests complete. If you=E2=80=99ve ever used the Instagram iPhone app you might notice that it does something sim= ilar, presenting an interface with the last available data whilst it makes = background API calls to get the latest.

Use

The initial cache hit triggers all of the same events (add,= reset, change etc) as an actual call to fe= tch, but the success callback is not fired until the AJAX call compl= etes. Instead a new callback is available, prefillSuccess:

myModel.fetch({
  prefill: tru=
e,
  prefillSuccess: someCallback, // Fires when the cache hit happens
  success: anotherCallback // Fires after the AJAX call
});

If you prefer working with promises, the call to fetch also= returns an instance of jQuery=E2=80=99s $.Deferred. A progress event is = triggered when the cache hit occurs and the promise is resolved when the AJ= AX call completes:

var<=
/span> modelPromise =3D myModel.fetch({ prefill<=
/span>: true });
modelPromise.progress(someCallback); // Fires wh=
en the cache hit happens
modelPromise.done(anotherCallback); // Fires aft=
er the AJAX call

All in all I think this is a pretty nice solution to the problem of a sl= ow initial page load for a single page JS application. As always the code a= nd tests are available on GitHub.

Backbone fetch cache

by Andy Appleton
Monday, 17 December 2012

Building a Backbone application which interacts with an exte= rnal API means a lot of calls to model.fetch() and colle= ction.fetch(). This can be pretty inefficient especially if the API = does not set cache headers on responses.

Backbone = fetch cache is a Backbone plugin which overrides the fetch method on Ba= ckbone models and collections to implement caching. Just include the script= on your page and start calling fetch with cache: true in the = options:

// Return cached data=
 if available, otherwise make an AJAX request
myModel.fetch({ cache: true });

By default responses are cached in memory under Backbone.fetchCach= e._cache and also persisted to localStorage if availabl= e. This can be disabled by setting:

// Cache in memory bu=
t not localStorage
Backbone.fetchCache.localStorage =3D false;

Cached items are saved for 5 minutes by default, but this is also config= urable by passing an expires option with a value in seconds to the fetch ca= ll:

// Expire this value =
in 1 minute
myModel.fetch({ cache: true, expires: 60 })

All options are passed through to the actual fetch call so you can still= register success and error callbacks for actual = AJAX requests. A promise is returned from the fake fetch method so the meth= od signature should be identical whether the data comes from a cache hit or= miss.

The code is all available on GitHub along with a full set of tests.

CoffeeScript is just JavaScript

by Andy Appleton
Monday, 03 December 2012

Most of the JavaScript I have written over the last year has= actually been CoffeeScript. It oc= curred to me the other day that I=E2=80=99m in the same mental mode whether= I=E2=80=99m writing JS or CS and I think that is because at it=E2=80=99s h= eart CoffeeScript is just a more succinct way to write the same language.

There is very little extra functionality that you get with CoffeeScript = that you couldn=E2=80=99t do with plain JS, it=E2=80=99s just nicer to writ= e. A good example is the class keyword, all it does is produce a constructor function wi= th methods on the prototype object =E2=80=93 easy enough with = JavaScript but more expressive and succinct with CoffeeScript.

clas=
s Bacon
  eat: -> 'nom'

Compiles to

func=
tion Bacon() {}

Bacon.prototype.eat =3D =
function() {
  return '=
nom';
};

If I were douchey enough I=E2=80=99d say it was like poetry. Actually I = am and it is.

I think the reason for this is that CoffeeScript doesn=E2=80=99t change = any of the fundamental rules of the language. this still behav= es the same way and you can still use call and apply to invoke functions however you like regardless of where they originate= .

There was a brief period where loops would fake a new scope with an inne= r function but happily that=E2=80=99s gone. In fact the only fundamental ch= ange I can think of is that variables are local to the current scope by def= ault and I think most people would agree that loosing var is a= n improvement.

For me this is why compile to JS languages like Dart and TypeSc= ript are completely uninteresting. I don=E2=80=99t want another languag= e, just a way to write the words function and prototype<= /code> a little less often and with fewer curly braces and semicolons.

Grunt Coffee Server

by Andy Appleton
Sunday, 18 November 2012

TL;DR I wrote a Grunt task to live compile CoffeeScript for local develop= ment.

Full story

Somewhat inspired by my colleague Phi= l=E2=80=99s tweet, I=E2=80=99ve written a G= runt plugin to make it easy to start writing any JavaScript project in = CoffeeScript.

It=E2=80=99s a simple static web server with a twist =E2=80=93 request a= .js file and it will be returned if it exists. If it doesn=E2=80=99t exist= then the server will try and find a .coffee version of the file and return= the compiled output if it can.

N.B. It does not deal with compilation for deployment, there=E2=80=99s already a plugin<= /a> for that.

Usage

This is packaged up as a Grunt task so you=E2=80=99ll need to install Gr= unt from NPM first (might as well do tha= t globally):

npm install -g grunt

Then install the task:

npm install grunt-coffee-server

Next you=E2=80=99ll need a gruntfile:

grunt init:gruntfile

Follow the instructions and take a look in your new grunt.js file. We need to register the new task by adding the following line:

grunt.loadNpmTasks('grunt-coffee-server'<=
/span>);

There are also a couple of configuration options available which are rea= d from the server config key:

grunt.initConfig({
  server: {
    port: 1337=
 // defaults to 3000 if not set
    base: './public' // defaults t=
o ./ if not set
  }
});

Finally run the server:

grunt coffee-server

I=E2=80=99ve written this kind of server manually a few times now for lo= cal development so I know it=E2=80=99ll be handy for me =E2=80=93 hopefully= it=E2=80=99ll be useful for you too.

It=E2=80=99s on GitHub so feel free to fork, pull request etc!

Using Grunt and Jasmine with AMD

by Andy Appleton
Wednesday, 03 October 2012

Since yesterday=E2=80=99s post on running = Jasmine specs with Grunt I have been working to integrate with a project wh= ich uses RequireJS. The Grunt Jasmine runner= task I am using does not support AMD specs so I have created a fork wh= ich is avail= able on GitHub.

To use it you will need to specify the repo URL in your package.js= on file=E2=80=A6

"depend=
encies": {
  "grunt=
-jasmine-runner": "<=
/span>git://github.com/mrappleton/grunt-jasmine-=
runner.git"
}

If the amd flag is set in the grunt.js config = file specs will be loaded via an AMD require call. This does not make an as= sumption about the AMD library being used, you must specify the path to tha= t in the helpers option. Here=E2=80=99s a full example gruntfile:

module.exports =3D function<=
/span>(grunt) {
  grunt.initConfig({
    jasmine : {
      amd: tru=
e,
      // Your Jasmine spec files
      specs : 'specs/**/*spec.js'=
,
      // Your spec helper files
      helpers: [
        '/path/to/require.js',
        '/path/to/requireConfig.j=
s'
      ],
    }
  });

  // Register tasks.
  grunt.loadNpmTasks('grunt-jasmin=
e-runner');

  // Default task.
  grunt.registerTask('default', 'jasmine');
};

This assumes that your spec files are AMD modules which require the sour= ce files as necessary, e.g.

define(['/src/myModule.js'], function(MyModule){
  describe('MyModule', function(){
    // etc...
  });
});

The spec runner HTML file will then require each spec file = in turn and execute the contents.

Summary

This is not the most flexible implementation, but it works for my use ca= se and I think is worth releasing. I opened a pull request on the main repo which was (quite reasonably) declined as they are apparently already work= ing on AMD support.

I=E2=80=99ll most probably switch back to the original version once AMD = support arrives but for now this fork will do nicely for me and I hope it c= an be useful to others.
